class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Written by: Nart Villeneuve, James T. Bennett</p>
<hr></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Rather than building custom malware, many threat actors behind targeted attacks use publicly or commercially available remote access Trojans (RATs). This pre-built malware has all the functionality needed to conduct cyber espionage and is controlled directly by humans, who have the ability to adapt to network defenses. As a result, the threat posed by these RATs should not be underestimated.</p>
<p>However, it is difficult to distinguish and correlate the activity of targeted threat actors based solely on their preference to use particular malware — especially, freely available malware. From an analyst’s perspective, it is unclear whether these actors choose to use this type of malware simply out of convenience or in a deliberate effort to blend in with traditional cybercrime groups, who also use these same tools.</p>
<p>There are numerous RATs available for free and for purchase in online forums, chat rooms and market places on the Internet. Most RATs are easy to use and thus attract novices. They are used for a variety of criminal activity, including “sextortion”. [1] The ubiquity of these RATs makes it difficult to determine if a particular security incident is related to a targeted threat, cybercrime or just a novice “script kiddie” causing a nuisance.</p>
<p>Although publicly available RATs are used by a variety of operators with different intents, the activity of particular threat actors can still be tracked by clustering command and control server information as well as the information that is set by the operators in the builder. These technical indicators, combined with context of an incident (such as the timing, specificity and human activity) allow analysts to assess the targeted or non-targeted nature of the threat.</p>
<p>In this post, we examine a publicly available RAT known as XtremeRAT. This malware has been used in targeted attacks as well as traditional cybercrime. During our investigation we found that the majority of XtremeRAT activity is associated with spam campaigns that typically distribute Zeus variants and other banking-focused malware. Why have these traditional cybercrime operators begun to distribute RATs? This seems odd, considering RATs require manual labor as opposed to automated banking Trojans.</p>
<p>Based on our observations we propose one or more of the following possible explanations:</p>
<ol>
<li>Smokescreen<br>The operations may be part of a targeted attack that seeks to disguise itself and its possible targets, by using spam services to launch the attacks.</li>
<li>Less traditional tools available<br>With more crimeware author arrests and/or disappearance of a number of banking Trojan developers, cybercriminals are resorting to using RATs to manually steal data, such as banking and credit card details. [2]</li>
<li>Complicated defenses require more versatile tools<br>As many traditional banking and financial institutions have improved their security practices, perhaps attackers have had a much more difficult time developing automation in their Trojans to cover all variations of these defenses; as such, RATs provide more versatility and effectiveness, at the expense of scalability.</li>
<li>Casting a wider net<br>After compromising indiscriminate targets, attackers may dig deeper into specific targets of interest and/or sell off the access rights of the victims’ systems and their data to others.</li>
</ol>
<p>These possible explanations are not mutually exclusive. One or all of them may be factors in explaining this observed activity.</p>
<p><strong>XtremeRAT</strong></p>
<p>The XtremeRAT was developed by “xtremecoder” and has been available since at least 2010. Written in Delphi, the code of XtremeRAT is shared amongst several other Delphi RAT projects including SpyNet, CyberGate, and Cerberus. The RAT is available for free; however, the developer charges 350 Euros for the source code. Unfortunately for xtremecoder, the source code has been leaked online. The current version is Xtreme 3.6, however, there are a variety of “private” version of this RAT available as well. As such, the official version of this RAT and its many variants are used by a wide variety of actors.</p>
<p>XtremeRAT allows an attacker to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Interact with the victim via a remote shell</li>
<li>Upload/download files</li>
<li>Interact with the registry</li>
<li>Manipulate running processes and services</li>
<li>Capture images of the desktop</li>
<li>Record from connected devices, such as a webcam or microphone</li>
</ul>
<p>Moreover, during the build process, the attacker can specify whether to include keylogging and USB infection functions.</p>
<p><strong>Extracting Intelligence</strong></p>
<p>XtremeRAT contains two components: a “client” and a “server”; however, from the attacker’s perspective, these terms have reversed meanings. Specifically, according to the author, the “server” component is the malware that resides on victim endpoints that connect to the “client”, which is operated by the attacker from one or more remote command-and-control (CnC) systems. Due to this confusing and overloaded terminology, we refer to the “server” as a “backdoor” on the victim and the “client” as a remote “controller” operated by the attacker.</p>
<p>XtremeRAT backdoors maintain and reference configuration data that was chosen by the attacker at the time they were built. This data can contain very useful hints to help group attacks and attribute them to actors, similar to what we have previously described in our Poison Ivy whitepaper. [3]</p>
<p>Several versions of XtremeRAT write this configuration data to disk under <code>%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Windows</code>, either directly, or to a directory named after mutex configured by the attacker. When written to disk, the data is RC4 encrypted with a key of either "CYBERGATEPASS" or "CONFIG" for the versions we have analyzed. In both cases, the key is Unicode. The config file has either a “.nfo” or ".cfg" extension depending on the version. XtremeRAT's key scheduling algorithm (KSA) implementation contains a bug wherein it only considers the length of the key string, not including the null bytes between each character, as is found in these Unicode strings. <em>As a result, it only effectively uses the first half of the key</em>. For example, the key “<code>C\x00O\x00N\x00F\x00I\x00G\x00</code>” is 12 bytes long, but the length is calculated as only being 6 bytes long. Because of this, the key that is ultimately used is “<code>C\x00O\x00N\x00</code>”.</p>
<p>The configuration data includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Name of the installed backdoor file</li>
<li>Directory under which the backdoor file is installed</li>
<li>Which process it will inject into (if specified)</li>
<li>CnC information</li>
<li>FTP information for sending stolen keystroke data to</li>
<li>Mutex name of the master process,</li>
<li>ID and group name which are used by the actors for organizational purposes</li>
</ul>
<p>Because the decrypted configuration data can be reliably located in memory (with only slight variations in its structure from version to version) and because not all versions of XtremeRAT will write their configuration data to disk, parsing memory dumps of infected systems is often the ideal method for extracting intelligence.</p>
<p>We are releasing python scripts we have developed to gather the configuration details for various versions of XtremeRAT from both process memory dumps and the encrypted configuration file on disk. The <a href="https://github.com/fireeye/tools/tree/master/malware/Xtreme%20RAT" rel="noopener" target="_blank">scripts are available here</a>.</p>
<p>Also included in this toolset is a script that decrypts and prints the contents of the log file created by XtremeRAT containing victim keystroke data. This log file is written to the same directory as the config file and has a “.dat” extension. Curiously, this log file is encrypted with a simple two-byte XOR instead of RC4. Later in this blog, we will share some of the configuration details we have extracted during our subsequent analysis.</p>
<p><strong>XtremeRAT Activity</strong></p>
<p>Using telemetry from the FireEye Dynamic Threat Intelligence (DTI) cloud, we examined 165 XtremeRAT samples from attacks that primarily hit the following sectors:</p>
<ul>
<li>Energy, utilities, and petroleum refining</li>
<li>Financial Services</li>
<li>High-tech</li>
</ul>
<p>These incidents include a spectrum of attacks including targeted attacks as well as indiscriminate attacks. Among these XtremeRAT-based attacks, we found that 4 of the 165 samples were used in targeted attacks against the High-Tech sector by threat actors we have called “MoleRats”. [4]</p>
<p><strong>Operation Molerats</strong></p>
<p>In 2012, XtremeRAT was used against a variety of governments as well as Israeli and Palestinian targets in what was known as Operation Molerats (the same attackers have also used variants of the Poison Ivy RAT). [5] Upon executing one particular sample (45142b17abd8a17a5e38305b718f3415), the malware beacons to “test.cable-modem.org” and “idf.blogsite.org”. In this particular case, the attacker used XtremeRAT 2.9 within a self-extracting archive that also presents a decoy document to the victim, where the decoy content appears to have been copied from a website.</p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" By default, the XtremeRAT controller sets the <strong>ID</strong> field as “Server” and <strong>Group</strong> field as “Servers”, with the default password used to authenticate, connect, and control a compromised endpoint as “1234567890”.</p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Figure 5. XtremeRAT controller connection settings</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>In the Figure 5, the attacker specified custom CnC servers and ports and changed the default password to “1411”. The attacker also changed the default process mutex name.</p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Figure 6: XtremeRat install settings</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>By default, the controller assigns a process mutex name of is “--((Mutex))--” and the attackers changed it to “fdgdfdg”. These indicators along with command and control domain names and the IP addresses that they resolve to can be used to cluster and track this activity over time.</p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Figure 7: Molerats cluster analysis</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>This is a cluster of Molerats activity. In addition to using the password “1411”, the attackers are also using the password “12345000”. This is a simple way to track the activity of these actors by using both passive DNS data and configuration information extracted from XtremeRAT.</p>
<p><strong>Spam Activity</strong></p>
<p>The vast majority of XtremeRAT activity clustered around the default password “1234567890” (116 samples). There was overlap between this large cluster and the second largest one which used the password “123456” (12 samples). The activity in these two clusters aligns with indicators observed in Spanish language spam runs. The “123456” cluster also contains spam in the English language, leveraging the recent tragedy in Kenya as a lure. [7]</p>
<p><strong>The Uranio Cluster</strong></p>
<p>In our sample set, we have 28 malware samples that connect to a set of sequentially numbered command and control servers:</p>
<ul>
<li>uranio.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio2.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio3.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio4.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio5.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio6.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>uranio7.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino-2.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino-4.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino-5.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino-8.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>platino-9.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>cometa3.no-ip.biz</li>
<li>cometa4.no-ip.biz</li>
</ul>
<p>The malware is being spammed out and has file names such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Certificaciones De Pagos Nominas Parafiscales jpg 125420215 58644745574455 .exe</li>
<li>Soportes de pagos certificaciones y documentos mes mayo 30 2013 567888885432235678888888123456.exe</li>
<li>Certificaciones De Pago Y Para Fiscales.exe</li>
</ul>

<p>“Server”, “Servers”, and “--((Mutex))--” are the defaults in the XtremeRAT controller for ID, Group, and Mutex respectively. The random mutex names in the table above can be generated by double-clicking in the Mutex field within the controller. In most cases, the number at the end of the group label is the same number used at the end of the subdomain for the CnC. In the case of “Uranio2011”, the subdomain is simply “uranio” and 2011 represents the port number used to communicate with the CnC infrastructure.</p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p>Figure 8: Portugese version of XtremeRAT controller</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" data-is-cws-richtext="true" class=" "><p><strong>Uranio Sinkhole Analysis</strong></p>
<p>We sinkholed uranio2.no-ip.biz between November 22, 2013 and January 6, 2014. During that time, 12000 unique IPs connected to the uranio2.no-ip.biz. Recall, that this number reflects only one of many command and control servers. [8]</p>
<p>However, estimating the number of victims this way is difficult due to DHCP lease times, which inflate the numbers, and NAT connections, which deflate the numbers. [9] As such, we counted the unique IP addresses that connected to the sinkhole on each day. The highest number of connections to this sinkhole was on Dec. 3, 2013 with 2003 connections and the lowest was Jan. 6, 2014 with 109 connections. The average number of unique IP addresses that connected to the sinkhole per day was 657.</p>
<p>While these IP addresses were in ranges assigned to 40 distinct countries, the vast majority of the connections to the sinkhole (92.7 percent) were from Colombia. Argentina was a distant second with 1.22 percent, followed by Venezuela with 1.02 percent, Egypt with 0.95 percent and the U.S. with 0.9 percent.</p>
<p><strong>Conclusion</strong></p>
<p>Determining the activity of targeted threat actors is difficult. Most of the activity associated with publicly available RATs is traditional cybercrime associated with spam runs, banking Trojans and malware distribution. However, useful indicators can be extracted from these ubiquitous RATs to track the activities of targeted threat actors (as well as cybercrime).</p>
<p><strong>Tools</strong></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://github.com/fireeye/tools/tree/master/malware/Xtreme%20RAT" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Xtreme RAT</a></li>
</ul>

type\u003d\"application/ld+json\"\u003e{\"@context\":\"https://schema.org\",\"@type\":\"BlogPosting\",\"@id\":\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/xtremerat-nuisance-or-threat\",\"headline\":\"XtremeRAT: Nuisance or Threat? | Mandiant\",\"description\":\"\",\"image\":\"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/03_ThreatIntelligenceWebsiteBannerIdeas_BA.max-2600x2600.png\",\"author\":[{\"@type\":\"Person\",\"name\":\"Mandiant Intelligence\",\"Security \\u0026 Identity\"],\"timeRequired\":\"PT13M\"}\u003c/script\u003e"],["XtremeRAT: Nuisance or Threat?"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Nart Villeneuve, James T. Bennett\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eRather than building custom malware, many threat actors behind targeted attacks use publicly or commercially available remote access Trojans (RATs). This pre-built malware has all the functionality needed to conduct cyber espionage and is controlled directly by humans, who have the ability to adapt to network defenses. As a result, the threat posed by these RATs should not be underestimated.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, it is difficult to distinguish and correlate the activity of targeted threat actors based solely on their preference to use particular malware — especially, freely available malware. From an analyst’s perspective, it is unclear whether these actors choose to use this type of malware simply out of convenience or in a deliberate effort to blend in with traditional cybercrime groups, who also use these same tools.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere are numerous RATs available for free and for purchase in online forums, chat rooms and market places on the Internet. Most RATs are easy to use and thus attract novices. They are used for a variety of criminal activity, including “sextortion”. [1] The ubiquity of these RATs makes it difficult to determine if a particular security incident is related to a targeted threat, cybercrime or just a novice “script kiddie” causing a nuisance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlthough publicly available RATs are used by a variety of operators with different intents, the activity of particular threat actors can still be tracked by clustering command and control server information as well as the information that is set by the operators in the builder. These technical indicators, combined with context of an incident (such as the timing, specificity and human activity) allow analysts to assess the targeted or non-targeted nature of the threat.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn this post, we examine a publicly available RAT known as XtremeRAT. This malware has been used in targeted attacks as well as traditional cybercrime. During our investigation we found that the majority of XtremeRAT activity is associated with spam campaigns that typically distribute Zeus variants and other banking-focused malware. Why have these traditional cybercrime operators begun to distribute RATs? This seems odd, considering RATs require manual labor as opposed to automated banking Trojans.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBased on our observations we propose one or more of the following possible explanations:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSmokescreen\u003cbr\u003eThe operations may be part of a targeted attack that seeks to disguise itself and its possible targets, by using spam services to launch the attacks.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLess traditional tools available\u003cbr\u003eWith more crimeware author arrests and/or disappearance of a number of banking Trojan developers, cybercriminals are resorting to using RATs to manually steal data, such as banking and credit card details. [2]\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eComplicated defenses require more versatile tools\u003cbr\u003eAs many traditional banking and financial institutions have improved their security practices, perhaps attackers have had a much more difficult time developing automation in their Trojans to cover all variations of these defenses; as such, RATs provide more versatility and effectiveness, at the expense of scalability.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCasting a wider net\u003cbr\u003eAfter compromising indiscriminate targets, attackers may dig deeper into specific targets of interest and/or sell off the access rights of the victims’ systems and their data to others.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThese possible explanations are not mutually exclusive. One or all of them may be factors in explaining this observed activity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eXtremeRAT\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe XtremeRAT was developed by “xtremecoder” and has been available since at least 2010. Written in Delphi, the code of XtremeRAT is shared amongst several other Delphi RAT projects including SpyNet, CyberGate, and Cerberus. The RAT is available for free; however, the developer charges 350 Euros for the source code. Unfortunately for xtremecoder, the source code has been leaked online. The current version is Xtreme 3.6, however, there are a variety of “private” version of this RAT available as well. As such, the official version of this RAT and its many variants are used by a wide variety of actors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eXtremeRAT allows an attacker to:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eInteract with the victim via a remote shell\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUpload/download files\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eInteract with the registry\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eManipulate running processes and services\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCapture images of the desktop\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eRecord from connected devices, such as a webcam or microphone\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMoreover, during the build process, the attacker can specify whether to include keylogging and USB infection functions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExtracting Intelligence\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eXtremeRAT contains two components: a “client” and a “server”; however, from the attacker’s perspective, these terms have reversed meanings. Specifically, according to the author, the “server” component is the malware that resides on victim endpoints that connect to the “client”, which is operated by the attacker from one or more remote command-and-control (CnC) systems. Due to this confusing and overloaded terminology, we refer to the “server” as a “backdoor” on the victim and the “client” as a remote “controller” operated by the attacker.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eXtremeRAT backdoors maintain and reference configuration data that was chosen by the attacker at the time they were built. This data can contain very useful hints to help group attacks and attribute them to actors, similar to what we have previously described in our Poison Ivy whitepaper. [3]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSeveral versions of XtremeRAT write this configuration data to disk under \u003ccode\u003e%APPDATA%\\Microsoft\\Windows\u003c/code\u003e, either directly, or to a directory named after mutex configured by the attacker. When written to disk, the data is RC4 encrypted with a key of either \"CYBERGATEPASS\" or \"CONFIG\" for the versions we have analyzed. In both cases, the key is Unicode. The config file has either a “.nfo” or \".cfg\" extension depending on the version. XtremeRAT's key scheduling algorithm (KSA) implementation contains a bug wherein it only considers the length of the key string, not including the null bytes between each character, as is found in these Unicode strings. \u003cem\u003eAs a result, it only effectively uses the first half of the key\u003c/em\u003e. For example, the key “\u003ccode\u003eC\\x00O\\x00N\\x00F\\x00I\\x00G\\x00\u003c/code\u003e” is 12 bytes long, but the length is calculated as only being 6 bytes long. Because of this, the key that is ultimately used is “\u003ccode\u003eC\\x00O\\x00N\\x00\u003c/code\u003e”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe configuration data includes:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eName of the installed backdoor file\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eDirectory under which the backdoor file is installed\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWhich process it will inject into (if specified)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCnC information\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFTP information for sending stolen keystroke data to\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMutex name of the master process,\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eID and group name which are used by the actors for organizational purposes\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBecause the decrypted configuration data can be reliably located in memory (with only slight variations in its structure from version to version) and because not all versions of XtremeRAT will write their configuration data to disk, parsing memory dumps of infected systems is often the ideal method for extracting intelligence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe are releasing python scripts we have developed to gather the configuration details for various versions of XtremeRAT from both process memory dumps and the encrypted configuration file on disk. The \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/fireeye/tools/tree/master/malware/Xtreme%20RAT\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003escripts are available here\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlso included in this toolset is a script that decrypts and prints the contents of the log file created by XtremeRAT containing victim keystroke data. This log file is written to the same directory as the config file and has a “.dat” extension. Curiously, this log file is encrypted with a simple two-byte XOR instead of RC4. Later in this blog, we will share some of the configuration details we have extracted during our subsequent analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eXtremeRAT Activity\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUsing telemetry from the FireEye Dynamic Threat Intelligence (DTI) cloud, we examined 165 XtremeRAT samples from attacks that primarily hit the following sectors:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eEnergy, utilities, and petroleum refining\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFinancial Services\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eHigh-tech\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThese incidents include a spectrum of attacks including targeted attacks as well as indiscriminate attacks. Among these XtremeRAT-based attacks, we found that 4 of the 165 samples were used in targeted attacks against the High-Tech sector by threat actors we have called “MoleRats”. [4]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOperation Molerats\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn 2012, XtremeRAT was used against a variety of governments as well as Israeli and Palestinian targets in what was known as Operation Molerats (the same attackers have also used variants of the Poison Ivy RAT). [5] Upon executing one particular sample (45142b17abd8a17a5e38305b718f3415), the malware beacons to “test.cable-modem.org” and “idf.blogsite.org”. In this particular case, the attacker used XtremeRAT 2.9 within a self-extracting archive that also presents a decoy document to the victim, where the decoy content appears to have been copied from a website.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Contents of SFX archive containing XtremeRAT\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat1.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat1.max-800x800.png to interact with systems compromised with XtremeRAT. In this case, it appears the actor used the \u003cstrong\u003eID\u003c/strong\u003e field to record the type of attack delivered (docx) and the \u003cstrong\u003eGroup\u003c/strong\u003e field was used to record a “campaign code” (IDF), which helps the actor keep track of the set of victims that were attacked during this campaign.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: XtremeRAT controller GUI\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat4_yxqn.max-1600x1600.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat4_yxqn.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat4_yxqn.max-1600x1600.png 1506w"," 1060px,  1506px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat4_yxqn.max-1600x1600.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThe attacker modified the highlighted information at build time. By default, the XtremeRAT controller sets the \u003cstrong\u003eID\u003c/strong\u003e field as “Server” and \u003cstrong\u003eGroup\u003c/strong\u003e field as “Servers”, with the default password used to authenticate, connect, and control a compromised endpoint as “1234567890”.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 5. XtremeRAT controller connection settings\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png 1118w"," 1060px,  1118px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat5_hwct.max-1200x1200.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eIn the Figure 5, the attacker specified custom CnC servers and ports and changed the default password to “1411”. The attacker also changed the default process mutex name.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: XtremeRat install settings\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png 1508w"," 1060px,  1508px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat6_gyze.max-1600x1600.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eBy default, the controller assigns a process mutex name of is “--((Mutex))--” and the attackers changed it to “fdgdfdg”. These indicators along with command and control domain names and the IP addresses that they resolve to can be used to cluster and track this activity over time.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 7: Molerats cluster analysis\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png 1496w"," 1060px,  1496px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat7_ejeb.max-1500x1500.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThis is a cluster of Molerats activity. In addition to using the password “1411”, the attackers are also using the password “12345000”. This is a simple way to track the activity of these actors by using both passive DNS data and configuration information extracted from XtremeRAT.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSpam Activity\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe vast majority of XtremeRAT activity clustered around the default password “1234567890” (116 samples). There was overlap between this large cluster and the second largest one which used the password “123456” (12 samples). The activity in these two clusters aligns with indicators observed in Spanish language spam runs. The “123456” cluster also contains spam in the English language, leveraging the recent tragedy in Kenya as a lure. [7]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThe Uranio Cluster\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn our sample set, we have 28 malware samples that connect to a set of sequentially numbered command and control servers:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio2.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio3.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio4.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio5.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio6.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003euranio7.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino-2.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino-4.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino-5.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino-8.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eplatino-9.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ecometa3.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ecometa4.no-ip.biz\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe malware is being spammed out and has file names such as:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCertificaciones De Pagos Nominas Parafiscales jpg 125420215 58644745574455 .exe\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSoportes de pagos certificaciones y documentos mes mayo 30 2013 567888885432235678888888123456.exe\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eCertificaciones De Pago Y Para Fiscales.exe\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe extracted the configurations for a sampling of the XtremeRAT samples we came across in this spam run and found the following results:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv “Servers”, and “--((Mutex))--” are the defaults in the XtremeRAT controller for ID, Group, and Mutex respectively. The random mutex names in the table above can be generated by double-clicking in the Mutex field within the controller. In most cases, the number at the end of the group label is the same number used at the end of the subdomain for the CnC. In the case of “Uranio2011”, the subdomain is simply “uranio” and 2011 represents the port number used to communicate with the CnC infrastructure.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 8: Portugese version of XtremeRAT controller\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png 1500w"," 1060px,  1500px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/xtremerat8_bibf.max-1500x1500.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUranio Sinkhole Analysis\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe sinkholed uranio2.no-ip.biz between November 22, 2013 and January 6, 2014. During that time, 12000 unique IPs connected to the uranio2.no-ip.biz. Recall, that this number reflects only one of many command and control servers. [8]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, estimating the number of victims this way is difficult due to DHCP lease times, which inflate the numbers, and NAT connections, which deflate the numbers. [9] As such, we counted the unique IP addresses that connected to the sinkhole on each day. The highest number of connections to this sinkhole was on Dec. 3, 2013 with 2003 connections and the lowest was Jan. 6, 2014 with 109 connections. The average number of unique IP addresses that connected to the sinkhole per day was 657.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile these IP addresses were in ranges assigned to 40 distinct countries, the vast majority of the connections to the sinkhole (92.7 percent) were from Colombia. Argentina was a distant second with 1.22 percent, followed by Venezuela with 1.02 percent, Egypt with 0.95 percent and the U.S. with 0.9 percent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConclusion\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDetermining the activity of targeted threat actors is difficult. Most of the activity associated with publicly available RATs is traditional cybercrime associated with spam runs, banking Trojans and malware distribution.
